<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Private spaceflight</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For paying space tourists, see <a href="/wiki/Space_tourism" title="Space tourism">Space tourism</a>. For general commercial use of space, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commercialization_of_space" title="Commercialization of space">Commercialization of space</a>. For entrepreneurial space ventures and colonization, see <a href="/wiki/NewSpace" title="NewSpace">NewSpace</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Satellites_For_Sale_-_GPN-2000-001036.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1985" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Satellites_For_Sale_-_GPN-2000-001036.jpg/220px-Satellites_For_Sale_-_GPN-2000-001036.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Satellites_For_Sale_-_GPN-2000-001036.jpg/330px-Satellites_For_Sale_-_GPN-2000-001036.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Satellites_For_Sale_-_GPN-2000-001036.jpg/440px-Satellites_For_Sale_-_GPN-2000-001036.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Satellites_For_Sale_-_GPN-2000-001036.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Astronaut <a href="/wiki/Dale_Gardner" title="Dale Gardner">Dale A. Gardner</a> holding a "For Sale" sign</div></div></div>
<p><b>Private spaceflight</b> is flight beyond the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A1rm%C3%A1n_line" title="Kármán line">Kármán line</a> (above the nominal edge of space at 100 km (62 mi) Earth <a href="/wiki/Altitude" title="Altitude">altitude</a>)—or the <a href="/wiki/New_product_development" title="New product development">development</a> of new spaceflight technology—that is conducted and paid for by an entity other than a government agency.
</p><p>In the early decades of the <a href="/wiki/Space_Age" title="Space Age">Space Age</a>, the government <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_agency" title="Space agency">space agencies</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> pioneered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_technology" title="Space technology">space technology</a> in collaboration with affiliated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Design_bureau" title="Design bureau">design bureaus</a> in the USSR and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Private_enterprise" title="Private enterprise">private companies</a> in the US, entirely funding both the development of new spaceflight technologies and the operational costs of spaceflight. The <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> was formed in 1975, largely following the same model of space technology development.
</p><p>Later on, large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Defense_contractor" title="Defense contractor">defense contractors</a> began to develop and operate space <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Launch_system" title="Launch system">launch systems</a>, derived from government rockets. Private spaceflight in Earth orbit includes <a href="/wiki/Communications_satellite" title="Communications satellite">communications satellites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Satellite_television" title="Satellite television">satellite television</a>, <a href="/wiki/Satellite_radio" title="Satellite radio">satellite radio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">astronaut transport</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sub-orbital" title="Sub-orbital">sub-orbital</a> and orbital <a href="/wiki/Space_tourism" title="Space tourism">space tourism</a>.
</p><p>In the 2000s, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Entrepreneur" title="Entrepreneur">entrepreneurs</a> began designing—and by the 2010s, deploying—space systems <a href="/wiki/Competition_(economics)" title="Competition (economics)">competitive</a> to the national-<a href="/wiki/Monopoly" title="Monopoly">monopoly</a> <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">governmental</a> systems<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gm20120205_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-gm20120205-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wapo20160819_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-wapo20160819-2">[2]</a></sup> of the early decades of the space age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ieee201201_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-ieee201201-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-belfiore2007_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-belfiore2007-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>7</span></sup>  These new offerings have brought about significant <a href="/wiki/Space_launch_market_competition" title="Space launch market competition">market competition in space launch services</a> after 2010 that had not been present previously.
</p><p>Successes to date include flying <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suborbital" title="Suborbital">suborbital</a> <a href="/wiki/Spaceplane" title="Spaceplane">spaceplanes</a>, launching <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbital</a> <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rockets</a>, flying two orbital <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expandable_space_habitat" title="Expandable space habitat">expandable</a> test modules (<a href="/wiki/Genesis_I" title="Genesis I">Genesis I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genesis_II" title="Genesis II">II</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/SpaceX_reusable_launch_system_development_program" title="SpaceX reusable launch system development program">successful development</a> of first-stage orbital launch vehicles that are able to <a href="/wiki/VTVL" title="VTVL">vertically land</a> after a launch so as to enable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reusable_launch_vehicle" title="Reusable launch vehicle">reuse</a>. The most powerful rocket in operation as of 2018, the <a href="/wiki/Falcon_Heavy" title="Falcon Heavy">Falcon Heavy</a>, was privately developed.
</p><p>Planned private spaceflights beyond Earth orbit include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tourism_on_Moon" title="Tourism on Moon">personal spaceflights around the Moon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Two private <a href="/wiki/Space_habitat" title="Space habitat">orbital habitat</a> prototypes are already in Earth orbit, with larger versions to follow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Planned private spaceflights beyond Earth orbit include <a href="/wiki/Solar_sail" title="Solar sail">solar sailing</a> prototypes (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LightSail-3" title="LightSail-3">LightSail-3</a>).
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_commercial_space_transportation">History of commercial space transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History of commercial space transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_private_spaceflight" title="Timeline of private spaceflight">Timeline of private spaceflight</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Proton_Zvezda_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2134" data-file-width="1466" decoding="async" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Proton_Zvezda_crop.jpg/220px-Proton_Zvezda_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Proton_Zvezda_crop.jpg/330px-Proton_Zvezda_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Proton_Zvezda_crop.jpg/440px-Proton_Zvezda_crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Proton_Zvezda_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Launch of a Proton rocket</div></div></div>
<p>In a 2012 article by <a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_News" title="Bloomberg News">Bloomberg News</a>, author Michael Burgan asserted that there is a "grand tradition of private wealth furthering advances in rocketry and space exploration" dating back to the early rocketry experiments of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Goddard" title="Robert Goddard">Robert Goddard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bb20120718_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-bb20120718-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite those earlier private undertakings, during the principal period of <a href="/wiki/Spaceflight" title="Spaceflight">spaceflight</a> in the mid-twentieth century, only <a href="/wiki/Nation_state" title="Nation state">nation states</a> developed and flew spacecraft above the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A1rm%C3%A1n_line" title="Kármán line">Kármán line</a>, the nominal boundary of space.  Spaceflight was thus the <a href="/wiki/Monopoly" title="Monopoly">monopoly</a> province of a small group of national governments.
</p><p>Both the <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">U.S. civilian space program</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soviet_space_program" title="Soviet space program">Soviet space program</a> were operated using mainly military pilots as <a href="/wiki/Astronaut" title="Astronaut">astronauts</a>. During this period, no commercial space launches were available to private operators, and no private organization was able to offer space launches. Eventually, private organizations were able to both offer and purchase space launches, thus beginning the period of private spaceflight.
</p><p>The first phase of private space operation was the launch of the first commercial <a href="/wiki/Communications_satellite" title="Communications satellite">communications satellites</a>. The U.S. <a href="/wiki/Communications_Satellite_Act_of_1962" title="Communications Satellite Act of 1962">Communications Satellite Act of 1962</a> opened the way to commercial consortia owning and operating their own satellites, although these were still launched on state-owned launch vehicles.
</p><p>In 1980, the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> created <a href="/wiki/Arianespace" title="Arianespace">Arianespace</a>, a company to be operated commercially after initial hardware and launch facilities were developed with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Government" title="European Government">government</a> funding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stAriane20130301_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-stAriane20130301-8">[8]</a></sup>  By 1995 Arianespace lofted its 100th <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellite</a> and by 1997 the Ariane rocket had its 100th launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Arianespace's 23 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shareholders" title="Shareholders">shareholders</a> represent scientific, technical, financial and political entities from 10 different <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>The history of full private space transportation includes early efforts by <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">German</a> company <a href="/wiki/OTRAG" title="OTRAG">OTRAG</a> in the 20th century, and numerous modern orbital and suborbital launch systems in the 21st century. More recent commercial spaceflight projects include the suborbital flights of <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Galactic" title="Virgin Galactic">Virgin Galactic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blue_Origin" title="Blue Origin">Blue Origin</a>, the orbital flights of <a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Orbital_Transportation_Services" title="Commercial Orbital Transportation Services">COTS</a> participants.
</p><p>Development of alternatives to government-provided <a href="/wiki/Space_launch" title="Space launch">space launch</a> services began in earnest in the 2000s.  Private interests began funding limited development programs, but the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">US government</a> later sponsored a <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Orbital_Transportation_Services" title="Commercial Orbital Transportation Services">series of</a> <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Crew_Development" title="Commercial Crew Development">programs</a> to incentivize and encourage private companies to begin offering both cargo, and later, crew <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_transport" title="Space transport">space transportation</a> services.
</p><p>Lower prices for launch services after 2010, and published prices for standard launch services, have brought about significant <a href="/wiki/Space_launch_market_competition" title="Space launch market competition">space launch market competition</a> that had not been present previously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vance2015_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-vance2015-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc20141203_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc20141203-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fp20131209_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-fp20131209-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj20150917_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-wsj20150917-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wapo20160819_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-wapo20160819-2">[2]</a></sup> By 2012, a private company had begun transporting cargo to and from the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> (ISS), while a second private company was scheduled to begin making deliveries in 2013, ushering in a time of regular private space cargo delivery to and return from the government-owned space facility in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low-Earth_orbit" title="Low-Earth orbit">low-Earth orbit</a> (LEO).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wired20121227_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-wired20121227-15">[15]</a></sup> In this new paradigm for LEO cargo transport, the government contracts for and pays for cargo services on substantially privately developed <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">space vehicles</a> rather than the government operating each of the cargo vehicles and <a href="/wiki/Space_logistics" title="Space logistics">cargo delivery systems.</a> As of  2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there is a <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_space_station_cargo_vehicles" title="Comparison of space station cargo vehicles">mix of private and government resupply vehicles</a> being used for the ISS, as the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Russia" title="Government of Russia">Russian</a> <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_(spacecraft)" title="Soyuz (spacecraft)">Soyuz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Progress_(spacecraft)" title="Progress (spacecraft)">Progress</a> vehicles, and the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> (ESA) <a href="/wiki/Automated_Transfer_Vehicle" title="Automated Transfer Vehicle">ATV</a> (through 2014) and the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Japan" title="Government of Japan">Japanese</a> <a href="/wiki/H-II_Transfer_Vehicle" title="H-II Transfer Vehicle">Kounotori</a> (through 2021) remain in operation after the 2011 retirement of the US <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a>.
</p><p>In June 2013, <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">British</a> newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">The Independent</a></i> claimed that "the space race is flaring back into life, and it's not massive institutions such as NASA that are in the running. The old view that human space flight is so complex, difficult and expensive that only huge government agencies could hope to accomplish it is being disproved by a new breed of flamboyant space privateers, who are planning to send humans out beyond the Earth's orbit for the first time since 1972,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph20130611_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph20130611-16">[16]</a></sup> particularly noting projects underway by <a href="/wiki/Mars_One" title="Mars One">Mars One</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inspiration_Mars_Foundation" title="Inspiration Mars Foundation">Inspiration Mars Foundation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bigelow_Aerospace" title="Bigelow Aerospace">Bigelow Aerospace</a> and <a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph20130611_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph20130611-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="American_deregulation">American deregulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: American deregulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="box-POV-check plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Ambox scales.svg" data-file-height="620" data-file-width="620" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ambox_scales.svg/40px-Ambox_scales.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ambox_scales.svg/60px-Ambox_scales.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ambox_scales.svg/80px-Ambox_scales.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>has been nominated to be checked for its <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">neutrality</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Discussion of this nomination can be found on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Private_spaceflight" title="Talk:Private spaceflight">talk page</a>.</span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">January 2016</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Space_Launch_Act_of_1984" title="Commercial Space Launch Act of 1984">Commercial Space Launch Act of 1984</a> required encouragement of commercial space ventures, adding a new clause to NASA's <a href="/wiki/Mission_statement" title="Mission statement">mission statement</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>(c) Commercial Use of Space.--Congress declares that the general welfare of the United States requires that the Administration seek and encourage, to the maximum extent possible, the fullest commercial use of space.</dd></dl>
<p>Yet one of NASA's early actions was to effectively ban private space flight through a mountain of red tape. From the beginning, though, this met significant opposition not only by the private sector, but in Congress. In 1962, Congress passed its first law pushing back the prohibition on private involvement in space, the <a href="/wiki/Communications_Satellite_Act_of_1962" title="Communications Satellite Act of 1962">Communications Satellite Act of 1962</a>. While largely focusing on the satellites of its namesake, this was described by both the law's opponents <i>and</i> advocates of private space, as the first step on the road to privatization.
</p><p>While launch vehicles were originally bought from private contractors, from the beginning of the Shuttle program until the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger" title="Space Shuttle Challenger">Challenger</a> disaster in 1986, NASA attempted to position its shuttle as the sole legal space launch option.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uscbo19861001_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-uscbo19861001-17">[17]</a></sup>
But with the mid-launch explosion/loss of <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger" title="Space Shuttle Challenger">Challenger</a> came the suspension of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">government</a>-operated shuttle flights, allowing the formation of a commercial launch industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bromberg1999_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-bromberg1999-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 30 October 1984, US President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> signed into law the <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Space_Launch_Act_of_1984" title="Commercial Space Launch Act of 1984">Commercial Space Launch Act</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csla1984_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-csla1984-19">[19]</a></sup> This enabled an American industry of private operators of <a href="/wiki/Expendable_launch_system" title="Expendable launch system">expendable launch systems</a>. Prior to the signing of this law, all commercial satellite launches in the United States were restricted by Federal regulation to NASA's <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_program" title="Space Shuttle program">Space Shuttle</a>.
</p><p>On 5 November 1990, United States President <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a> signed into law the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Launch_Services_Purchase_Act_of_1990" title="Launch Services Purchase Act of 1990">Launch Services Purchase Act</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The Act, in a complete reversal of the earlier Space Shuttle monopoly, ordered NASA to purchase launch services for its primary payloads from commercial providers whenever such services are required in the course of its activities.
</p><p>In 1996 the United States government selected <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin" title="Lockheed Martin">Lockheed Martin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> to each develop <a href="/wiki/Evolved_Expendable_Launch_Vehicle" title="Evolved Expendable Launch Vehicle">Evolved Expendable Launch Vehicles</a> (EELV) to compete for launch contracts and provide assured access to space. The government's acquisition strategy relied on the strong commercial viability of both vehicles to lower unit costs. This anticipated market demand did not materialize, but both the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Delta_IV_rocket" title="Delta IV rocket">Delta IV</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atlas_V" title="Atlas V">Atlas V</a> EELVs remain in active service.
</p><p>Commercial launches outnumbered government launches at the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Range" title="Eastern Range">Eastern Range</a> in 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commercial_Space_Act_of_1998" title="Commercial Space Act of 1998">Commercial Space Act</a> was passed in 1998 and implements many of the provisions of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Launch_Services_Purchase_Act_of_1990" title="Launch Services Purchase Act of 1990">Launch Services Purchase Act of 1990</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usg19981028_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-usg19981028-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nonetheless, until 2004 NASA kept private space flight effectively illegal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> But that year, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commercial_Space_Launch_Amendments_Act_of_2004" title="Commercial Space Launch Amendments Act of 2004">Commercial Space Launch Amendments Act of 2004</a> required that NASA and the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aviation_Administration" title="Federal Aviation Administration">Federal Aviation Administration</a> legalize private space flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbc2004_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-nbc2004-24">[24]</a></sup>
The 2004 Act also specified a "learning period" which restricted the ability of the FAA to enact regulations regarding the safety of people who might actually fly on commercial spacecraft through 2012, ostensibly because spaceflight participants would share the risk of flight through <a href="/wiki/Informed_consent" title="Informed consent">informed consent</a> procedures of human spaceflight risks, while requiring the launch provider to be legally liable for potential losses to uninvolved persons and structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsr20150526_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-tsr20150526-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>To the end of 2014, commercial passenger flights in space has remained effectively illegal, as the FAA has refused to give a commercial operator's license to any private space company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Government" title="United States Government">United States</a> updated US commercial space legislation with the passage of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SPACE_Act_of_2015" title="SPACE Act of 2015">SPACE Act of 2015</a> in November 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hr2262_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-hr2262-27">[27]</a></sup>
The full name of the act is <i>Spurring Private Aerospace Competitiveness and Entrepreneurship Act of 2015</i>
</p><p>The update US law explicitly allows "US citizens to engage in the commercial exploration and exploitation of 'space resources' [including ... water and minerals]." The right does not extend to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_life" title="Biological life">biological life</a>, so anything that is alive may not be exploited commercially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wired20151112_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-wired20151112-28">[28]</a></sup>  The Act further asserts that "the United States does not [(by this Act)] assert <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereignty</a>, or sovereign or <a href="/wiki/Exclusive_right" title="Exclusive right">exclusive rights</a> or jurisdiction over, or the ownership of, any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_body" title="Celestial body">celestial body</a>."
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wired20151112_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-wired20151112-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The SPACE Act includes the extension of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indemnification" title="Indemnification">indemnification</a> of US launch providers for extraordinary catastrophic third-party losses of a failed launch through 2025, while the previous indemnification law was scheduled to expire in 2016.  The Act also extends, through 2025, the "learning period" restrictions which limit the ability of the FAA to enact regulations regarding the safety of spaceflight <i>participants</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsr20150526_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-tsr20150526-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Indemnification for extraordinary third-party losses has, as of 2015, been a component of US space law for over 25 years, and during this time, "has never been invoked in any commercial launch mishap."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tsr20150526_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-tsr20150526-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Russian_privatization">Russian privatization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Russian privatization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_industry_of_Russia" title="Space industry of Russia">Space industry of Russia</a></div>
<p>In 1992, Resurs-500 capsule containing gifts was launched from <a href="/wiki/Plesetsk_Cosmodrome" title="Plesetsk Cosmodrome">Plesetsk Cosmodrome</a> in what was a private spaceflight called <a href="/wiki/Space_Flight_Europe-America_500" title="Space Flight Europe-America 500">Europe-America 500</a>. The flight was conceived by the Russian <a href="/wiki/Foundation_for_Social_Inventions" title="Foundation for Social Inventions">Foundation for Social Inventions</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Progress_State_Research_and_Production_Rocket_Space_Center" title="Progress State Research and Production Rocket Space Center">TsSKB-Progress</a>, a Russian rocket-building company, to increase trade between Russia and USA, and promote use of technology once reserved only for military forces. Money for the launch was raised from a collection of Russian companies. The capsule parachuted into the Pacific Ocean and was brought to Seattle by a Russian missile-tracking ship.
</p><p>The Russian government sold part of its stake in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S.P._Korolev_Rocket_and_Space_Corporation_Energia" title="S.P. Korolev Rocket and Space Corporation Energia">RSC Energia</a> to private investors in 1994. Energia together with Khrunichev constituted most of the Russian manned space program. In 1997, the Russian government sold off enough of its share to lose the majority position.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The text near this tag is dated. (December 2014)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_alliances">Launch alliances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Launch alliances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since 1995 Khrunichev's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_rocket" title="Proton rocket">Proton rocket</a> is marketed through <a href="/wiki/International_Launch_Services" title="International Launch Services">International Launch Services</a> while the <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_(rocket_family)" title="Soyuz (rocket family)">Soyuz rocket</a> is marketed via <a href="/wiki/Starsem" title="Starsem">Starsem</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Sea_Launch" title="Sea Launch">Sea Launch</a> project flies the Ukrainian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zenit_rocket" title="Zenit rocket">Zenit rocket</a>.
</p><p>In 2003 Arianespace joined with <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> Launch Services and <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Heavy_Industries" title="Mitsubishi Heavy Industries">Mitsubishi Heavy Industries</a> to create the <a href="/wiki/Launch_Services_Alliance" title="Launch Services Alliance">Launch Services Alliance</a>. In 2005, continued weak commercial demand for EELV launches drove Lockheed Martin and Boeing to propose a joint venture called the <a href="/wiki/United_Launch_Alliance" title="United Launch Alliance">United Launch Alliance</a> to service the United States government launch market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spaceflight_privatization">Spaceflight privatization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Spaceflight privatization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since the 1980s, various private initiatives have <a href="/wiki/Startup_company" title="Startup company">started up</a> to pursue the private use of <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">space</a>. The first privately funded rocket to achieve spaceflight was <a href="/wiki/Conestoga_(rocket)" title="Conestoga (rocket)">Conestoga I</a>, which was launched by <a href="/wiki/Space_Services_Inc." title="Space Services Inc.">Space Services Inc.</a> on a suborbital flight to 309 km (192 mi) altitude on 9 September 1982.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 2000s, several public-private partnerships were established in the United States to take advantage of <a href="/wiki/Entrepreneurship" title="Entrepreneurship">entrepreneurial</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_enterprise" title="Free enterprise">companies</a> to accelerate spaceflight technology development and reduce the cost of access to space, both for cargo and passengers <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_transport" title="Space transport">transport</a>. In addition, several purely private initiatives have begun in the 2010s to develop various aspects of space technology such as <a href="/wiki/SpaceX_reusable_launch_system_development_program" title="SpaceX reusable launch system development program">reusable launch systems</a> and private spaceflight endeavors to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_solar_system" title="Inner solar system">inner solar system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iac_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-iac-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 17 December 2003—on the 100th anniversary of the <a href="/wiki/Wright_brothers" title="Wright brothers">Wright brothers</a> first powered flight of an <a href="/wiki/Aircraft" title="Aircraft">aircraft</a>—<i><a href="/wiki/SpaceShipOne" title="SpaceShipOne">SpaceShipOne</a></i>, an experimental <a href="/wiki/Spaceplane" title="Spaceplane">spaceplane</a> piloted by <a href="/wiki/Brian_Binnie" title="Brian Binnie">Brian Binnie</a>, <a href="/wiki/SpaceShipOne_flight_11P" title="SpaceShipOne flight 11P">made</a> its first <a href="/wiki/Rocket_engine" title="Rocket engine">rocket-powered</a> flight, the first privately built craft to ever achieve supersonic flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-belfiore2007_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-belfiore2007-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>8</span></sup>  The next year, <i>SpaceShipOne</i> made three suborbital flights into space, becoming the first privately built and operated vehicle to achieve manned spaceflight.
</p><p>In 2006, NASA initiated a program to purchase commercial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_transport" title="Space transport">rides</a> to carry cargo to the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>, while funding a portion of the development of new technology in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public-private_partnership" title="Public-private partnership">public-private partnership</a>, initially with <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$500 million</span> of contracted development funds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-belfiore2007_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-belfiore2007-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>10</span></sup>
</p><p>On 1 February 2010, United States President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barack_Obama_space_policy_speech_at_Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Barack Obama space policy speech at Kennedy Space Center">proposed in a speech</a> that NASA exit the business of flying astronauts from Earth to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low-Earth_orbit" title="Low-Earth orbit">low-Earth orbit</a>—the locus of human spaceflight ever since the last Lunar manned mission in 1972—and move it to private companies who contract with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal Government of the United States">government</a> to provide <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Orbital_Transportation_Services" title="Commercial Orbital Transportation Services">cargo resupply</a> services to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISS" title="ISS">ISS</a>. The proposal acted on the findings of the 2009 <a href="/wiki/Review_of_United_States_Human_Space_Flight_Plans_Committee" title="Review of United States Human Space Flight Plans Committee">Augustine Commission</a> and built on the success of the <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Resupply_Services" title="Commercial Resupply Services">Commercial Resupply Services</a> that outsourced American cargo delivery to the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>'
</p><p>In May 2015, the Japanese legislature is considering legislation to allow private company spaceflight initiatives in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jn20150603_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-jn20150603-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The development of commercial space transportation will need <a href="/wiki/Airspace" title="Airspace">airspace</a> integration and <a href="/wiki/Air_traffic_management" title="Air traffic management">air traffic management</a> adaptation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Companies">Companies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Companies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_private_spaceflight_companies" title="List of private spaceflight companies">List of private spaceflight companies</a></div>
<p>Today many commercial space transportation companies offer launch services to satellite companies and government space organizations around the world. In 2005 there were 18 total commercial launches and 37 non-commercial launches. Russia flew 44% of commercial orbital launches, while Europe had 28% and the United States had 6%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> China's first private launch, a suborbital flight by <a href="/wiki/OneSpace" title="OneSpace">OneSpace</a>, took place in May 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn20180517_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnn20180517-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Funding">Funding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Funding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In recent years, the <a href="/wiki/Finance" title="Finance">funding</a> to support private spaceflight has begun to be raised from a larger pool of sources than the relatively more limited sources of the 1990s. For example, as of  June 2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> and in the United States alone, ten <a href="/wiki/Billionaire" title="Billionaire">billionaires</a> have made "serious investments in private spaceflight activities"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hc20130628_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-hc20130628-38">[38]</a></sup> at six companies, including <a href="/wiki/Stratolaunch_Systems" title="Stratolaunch Systems">Stratolaunch Systems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Planetary_Resources" title="Planetary Resources">Planetary Resources</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blue_Origin" title="Blue Origin">Blue Origin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Galactic" title="Virgin Galactic">Virgin Galactic</a>, <a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bigelow_Aerospace" title="Bigelow Aerospace">Bigelow Aerospace</a>. The ten investors are <a href="/wiki/Paul_Allen" title="Paul Allen">Paul Allen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larry_Page" title="Larry Page">Larry Page</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eric_E._Schmidt" title="Eric E. Schmidt">Eric E. Schmidt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ram_Shriram" title="Ram Shriram">Ram Shriram</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_Simony" title="Charles Simony">Charles Simony</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ross_Perot,_Jr." title="Ross Perot, Jr.">Ross Perot, Jr.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Bezos" title="Jeff Bezos">Jeff Bezos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Branson" title="Richard Branson">Richard Branson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elon_Musk" title="Elon Musk">Elon Musk</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bigelow" title="Robert Bigelow">Robert Bigelow</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>It is not yet clear to what extent these entrepreneurs see "legitimate business opportunity, [for example,] space tourism and other commercial activities in space, or [are] wealthy men seeking the exclusivity that space offers innovators and investors."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hc20130628_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-hc20130628-38">[38]</a></sup> These investments are a "gamble", and may, or may not pay off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hc20130628_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-hc20130628-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_launchers">Commercial launchers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Commercial launchers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_transport" title="Space transport">Space transport</a></div>
<p>The space transport business has, historically, had its primary customers in national governments and large commercial segments. Launches of government <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Payload_(air_and_space_craft)" title="Payload (air and space craft)">payloads</a>, including military, civilian and scientific satellites, was the largest market segment in 2007 at nearly $100 billion a year. This segment is dominated by domestic favorites such as the <a href="/wiki/United_Launch_Alliance" title="United Launch Alliance">United Launch Alliance</a> for U.S. government payloads and <a href="/wiki/Arianespace" title="Arianespace">Arianespace</a> for European satellites. The commercial payload segment, valued at under $3 billion a year, was dominated by <a href="/wiki/Arianespace" title="Arianespace">Arianespace</a> in 2007, with over 50% of the market segment,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> followed by Russian launchers. See a complete list of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_rockets#Launch_systems" title="List of rockets">launch systems</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="US_government_commercial_cargo_services">US government commercial cargo services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: US government commercial cargo services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="3402" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg/220px-COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg/330px-COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg/440px-COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:COTS2_Dragon_is_berthed.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/SpaceX_Dragon" title="SpaceX Dragon">SpaceX Dragon</a> berthing with the ISS during its <a href="/wiki/Dragon_C2%2B" title="Dragon C2+">final demonstration mission</a>, on 25 May 2012.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Orbital_Transportation_Services" title="Commercial Orbital Transportation Services">Commercial Orbital Transportation Services</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">US government</a> determined to begin a process to purchase <a href="/wiki/Space_launch" title="Space launch">orbital launch</a> services for cargo deliveries to the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> (ISS) beginning in the mid-2000s, rather than operate the launch and delivery services as they had with the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a>, which was to retire in less than half a decade, and ultimately did retire in 2011.  On 18 January 2006, NASA announced an opportunity for US commercial providers to demonstrate orbital transportation services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> As of 2008, NASA planned to spend $500 million through 2010 to finance development of <a href="/wiki/Private_sector" title="Private sector">private sector</a> capability to transport payloads to the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> (ISS).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The text near this tag is dated. (January 2013)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup> This was considered more challenging than then-available commercial space transportation because it would require precision <a href="/wiki/Orbit_insertion" title="Orbit insertion">orbit insertion</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In-orbit_rendezvous" title="In-orbit rendezvous">rendezvous</a> and possibly docking with another spacecraft. The commercial vendors competed in specific service areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 2006, NASA announced that two relatively young aerospace companies, <a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rocketplane_Kistler" title="Rocketplane Kistler">Rocketplane Kistler</a>, had been awarded $278 million and $207 million, respectively, under the COTS program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> In 2008, NASA anticipated that commercial <a href="/wiki/Space_logistics" title="Space logistics">cargo delivery services to and return services from</a> the ISS would be necessary through at least 2015. The NASA Administrator suggested that space transportation services procurement may be expanded to orbital <a href="/wiki/Propellant_depot" title="Propellant depot">fuel depots</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moon#Physical_characteristics" title="Moon">lunar surface</a> deliveries should the first phase of COTS prove successful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>After it transpired that Rocketplane Kistler was failing to meet its contractual deadlines, NASA terminated its contract with the company in August 2008, after only $32 million had been spent. Several months later, in December 2008, NASA awarded the remaining $170 million in that contract to <a href="/wiki/Orbital_Sciences_Corporation" title="Orbital Sciences Corporation">Orbital Sciences Corporation</a> to develop resupply services to the ISS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emerging_personal_spaceflight">Emerging personal spaceflight</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Emerging personal spaceflight">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_tourism" title="Space tourism">Space tourism</a></div>
<p>Before 2004, the year it was legalized in the US, no privately operated manned spaceflight had ever occurred. The only private individuals to journey to space went as <a href="/wiki/Space_tourism" title="Space tourism">space tourists</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> or on Russian <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_programme" title="Soyuz programme">Soyuz</a> flights to <a href="/wiki/Mir" title="Mir">Mir</a> or the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>.
</p><p>All private individuals who flew to space before <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Tito" title="Dennis Tito">Dennis Tito</a>'s self-financed International Space Station visit in 2001 had been sponsored by their home governments or by private corporations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Those trips include US Congressman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bill_Nelson_(politician)" title="Bill Nelson (politician)">Bill Nelson's</a> January 1986 flight on the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia" title="Space Shuttle Columbia">Space Shuttle Columbia</a> and Japanese television reporter <a href="/wiki/Toyohiro_Akiyama" title="Toyohiro Akiyama">Toyohiro Akiyama</a>'s 1990 flight to the <a href="/wiki/Mir" title="Mir">Mir</a> Space Station.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ansari_X_PRIZE" title="Ansari X PRIZE">Ansari X PRIZE</a> was intended to stimulate private investment in the development of spaceflight technologies. 21 June 2004, test flight of <a href="/wiki/SpaceShipOne" title="SpaceShipOne">SpaceShipOne</a>, a contender for the X PRIZE, was the first <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">human spaceflight</a> in a privately developed and operated vehicle.
</p><p>On 27 September 2004, following the success of SpaceShipOne, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Branson" title="Richard Branson">Richard Branson</a>, owner of <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Group" title="Virgin Group">Virgin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burt_Rutan" title="Burt Rutan">Burt Rutan</a>, SpaceShipOne's designer, announced that <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Galactic" title="Virgin Galactic">Virgin Galactic</a> had licensed the craft's technology, and were planning commercial space flights in 2.5 to 3 years. A fleet of five craft (<a href="/wiki/SpaceShipTwo" title="SpaceShipTwo">SpaceShipTwo</a>, launched from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WhiteKnightTwo" title="WhiteKnightTwo">WhiteKnightTwo</a> carrier airplane) were to be constructed, and flights would be offered at around $200,000 each, although Branson said he planned to use this money to make flights more affordable in the long term. A test flight of SpaceShipTwo crashed in October 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2004, United States President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> signed into law the Commercial Space Launch Amendments Act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The Act resolved the regulatory ambiguity surrounding private spaceflights and is designed to promote the development of the emerging U.S. commercial human space flight industry.
</p><p>On 12 July 2006, <a href="/wiki/Bigelow_Aerospace" title="Bigelow Aerospace">Bigelow Aerospace</a> launched the <i><a href="/wiki/Genesis_I" title="Genesis I">Genesis I</a></i>, a subscale pathfinder of an orbital space station module. <i><a href="/wiki/Genesis_II" title="Genesis II">Genesis II</a></i> was launched on 28 June 2007, and there are plans for additional prototypes to be launched in preparation for the production model <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BA_330" title="BA 330">BA 330</a></i> spacecraft.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The text near this tag is dated. (November 2010)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zero2infinity" title="Zero2infinity">Zero2infinity</a>, a Spanish aerospace company, is developing a <a href="/wiki/High-altitude_balloon" title="High-altitude balloon">high-altitude balloon</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Launch_vehicle" title="Launch vehicle">launch vehicle</a> termed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bloostar" title="Bloostar">bloostar</a> to launch small satellites to orbit for customers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Private_foundations">Private foundations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Private foundations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/B612_Foundation" title="B612 Foundation">B612 Foundation</a> is currently designing and building an <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a>-finding space telescope named <a href="/wiki/Sentinel_Space_Telescope" title="Sentinel Space Telescope">Sentinel</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B612-20120913_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-B612-20120913-49">[49]</a></sup> with plans to launch it in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B612-20120919_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-B612-20120919-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/The_Planetary_Society" title="The Planetary Society">The Planetary Society</a>, a nonprofit space research and advocacy organization, has sponsored a series of small satellites to test the feasibility of <a href="/wiki/Solar_sail" title="Solar sail">solar sailing</a>. Their first such project, <a href="/wiki/Cosmos_1" title="Cosmos 1">Cosmos 1</a>, was launched in 2005 but failed to reach space, and was succeeded by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lightsail-1" title="Lightsail-1">Lightsail</a> series, the first of which launched on 20 May 2015. A second spacecraft is expected to launch in 2016 on a more complex mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Copenhagen_Suborbitals" title="Copenhagen Suborbitals">Copenhagen Suborbitals</a> is a crowd funded amateur manned space programme. As of  2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> it has flown four home-built rockets and two mock-up space capsules.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Plans">Plans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Plans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Many have speculated on where private spaceflight may go in the near future. Numerous projects of orbital and suborbital launch systems for satellites and manned flights exist. Some orbital manned missions would be state-sponsored like most COTS participants. (that develop their own launch systems). Another possibility is for paid suborbital tourism on craft like those from <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Galactic" title="Virgin Galactic">Virgin Galactic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Space_Adventures" title="Space Adventures">Space Adventures</a>, <a href="/wiki/XCOR_Aerospace" title="XCOR Aerospace">XCOR Aerospace</a>, <a href="/wiki/RocketShip_Tours" title="RocketShip Tours">RocketShip Tours</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ARCASPACE" title="ARCASPACE">ARCASPACE</a>, <a href="/wiki/PlanetSpace" title="PlanetSpace">PlanetSpace</a>-<a href="/wiki/Canadian_Arrow" title="Canadian Arrow">Canadian Arrow</a>, British <a href="/wiki/Starchaser_Industries" title="Starchaser Industries">Starchaser Industries</a> or non-commercial like <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen_Suborbitals" title="Copenhagen Suborbitals">Copenhagen Suborbitals</a>. Additionally, suborbital spacecraft have applications for faster intercontinental package delivery and passenger flight.
</p>
<h3><span id="Private_orbital_spaceflight.2C_space_stations"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Private_orbital_spaceflight,_space_stations">Private orbital spaceflight, space stations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Private orbital spaceflight, space stations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Falcon_9" title="Falcon 9">Falcon 9</a> rocket, first launched in 2010 with no passengers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfn20100604_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-sfn20100604-52">[52]</a></sup> was designed to be subsequently <a href="/wiki/Human-rating_certification" title="Human-rating certification">human-rated</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Atlas_V" title="Atlas V">Atlas V</a>  launch vehicle is also a contender for human-rating.
</p><p>Plans and a full-scale prototype for the <a href="/wiki/SpaceX_Dragon" title="SpaceX Dragon">SpaceX Dragon</a>, a capsule capable of carrying up to 7 passengers, were announced in March 2006,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dragon_V2" title="Dragon V2">Dragon version 2</a> flight hardware was unveiled in May 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aw20140530_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-aw20140530-54">[54]</a></sup>
As of  September 2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, both SpaceX and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CST-100" title="CST-100">Boeing</a> have received contracts from NASA to complete building, testing, and flying up to six flights of human-rated space capsules to the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> beginning in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2010, SpaceX launched the second Falcon 9 and the first operational Dragon spacecraft. The mission was deemed fully successful, marking the first launch to space, atmospheric reentry and recovery of a capsule by a private company. Subsequent <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Orbital_Transportation_Services" title="Commercial Orbital Transportation Services">COTS</a> missions included increasingly complex orbital tasks, culminating in Dragon first docking to the ISS in 2012.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bigelow_Aerospace" title="Bigelow Aerospace">Bigelow Aerospace</a> develops <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BA_330" title="BA 330">BA 330</a> module (based on the former NASA <a href="/wiki/TransHab" title="TransHab">TransHab</a> design) intended to be used for activities like microgravity research, space manufacturing, and <a href="/wiki/Space_tourism" title="Space tourism">space tourism</a> with modules serving as orbital "hotels". To promote private manned launch efforts, Bigelow offered the US$50 million <a href="/wiki/America%27s_Space_Prize" title="America's Space Prize">America's Space Prize</a> for the first US-based privately funded team to launch a manned reusable spacecraft to orbit on or before 10 January 2010; such feat is yet to be achieved as of  December 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.
</p><p>The British Government partnered in 2015 with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ESA" title="ESA">ESA</a> to promote a possibly commercial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Single-stage_to_orbit" title="Single-stage to orbit">single-stage to orbit</a> <a href="/wiki/Spaceplane" title="Spaceplane">spaceplane</a> concept called <a href="/wiki/Skylon_(spacecraft)" title="Skylon (spacecraft)">Skylon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> This design was pioneered by the privately held <a href="/wiki/Reaction_Engines_Limited" title="Reaction Engines Limited">Reaction Engines Limited</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> a company founded by <a href="/wiki/Alan_Bond_(engineer)" title="Alan Bond (engineer)">Alan Bond</a> after <a href="/wiki/HOTOL" title="HOTOL">HOTOL</a> was canceled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReactionEngines1_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReactionEngines1-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2012, private company <a href="/wiki/NanoRacks" title="NanoRacks">NanoRacks</a> provides commercial access to the US National Laboratory space on the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> (ISS).  Science experiments can be conducted on a variety of standard rack-sized experimental platforms, with <a href="/wiki/International_standard" title="International standard">standard</a> interfaces for power and data acquisition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forbes20130304_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-forbes20130304-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On-orbit_propellant_depots">On-orbit propellant depots</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: On-orbit propellant depots">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Propellant_depot" title="Propellant depot">Propellant depot</a></div>
<p>In a presentation given 15 November 2005 to the 52nd Annual Conference of the <a href="/wiki/American_Astronautical_Society" title="American Astronautical Society">American Astronautical Society</a>, NASA Administrator <a href="/wiki/Michael_D._Griffin" title="Michael D. Griffin">Michael D. Griffin</a> suggested that establishing an on-orbit propellant depot is, "Exactly the type of enterprise which should be left to industry and to the marketplace."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> At the Space Technology and Applications International Forum in 2007, Dallas Bienhoff of <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> made a presentation detailing the benefits of propellant depots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shackleton_Energy_Company" title="Shackleton Energy Company">Shackleton Energy Company</a> has established operational plans, an extensive teaming and industrial consortium for developing LEO Propellant Depots supplied by Lunar polar sourced water ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ss20121214c_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-ss20121214c-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asteroid_mining">Asteroid mining</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Asteroid mining">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Asteroidmining.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="230" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Asteroidmining.jpg/220px-Asteroidmining.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Asteroidmining.jpg/330px-Asteroidmining.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d5/Asteroidmining.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Asteroidmining.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Asteroid mining spacecraft</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_mining" title="Asteroid mining">Asteroid mining</a></div>
<p>Some have speculated on the profitability of <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mining</a> <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroids" title="Asteroids">asteroids</a>. According to some estimates, a one kilometer-diameter asteroid would contain 30 million tons of nickel, 1.5 million tons of metal <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a> and 7,500tons of <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>; the platinum alone would have a value of more than $150 billion at 2008 terrestrial prices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_elevators">Space elevators</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Space elevators">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_elevator" title="Space elevator">Space elevator</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Space_elevator" title="Space elevator">space elevator</a> system is a possible launch system, currently under investigation by at least one private venture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> There are concerns over cost, general feasibility and some political issues. On the plus side the potential to scale the system to accommodate traffic would (in theory) be greater than some other alternatives. Some factions contend that a space elevator — if successful — would not supplant existing launch solutions but complement them.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Venture_capital_investment">Venture capital investment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Venture capital investment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Since 2005, there has been <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$10 billion</span> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Private_capital" title="Private capital">private capital</a> invested in the space sector,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ibt20151009_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-ibt20151009-66">[66]</a></sup>
most of it in the United States which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commercial_Space_Launch_Act" title="Commercial Space Launch Act">liberalized private space sector investment</a> beginning in the 1980s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csla1984_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-csla1984-19">[19]</a></sup>
with additional legislative reforms in the 1990s–2000s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bromberg1999_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-bromberg1999-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usg19981028_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-usg19981028-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbc2004_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-nbc2004-24">[24]</a></sup>  From 2000 through the end of 2015, a total of <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$13.3 billion</span> of investment finance has been invested in the space sector, with <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$2.9 billion</span> of that being <a href="/wiki/Venture_capital" title="Venture capital">venture capital</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fortune20160222_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-fortune20160222-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015 alone, venture capital firms invested <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$1.8 billion</span> in private spaceflight companies, more than they had in the previous 15 years combined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fortune20160222_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-fortune20160222-67">[67]</a></sup> As of  October 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the largest and most active investors in space were <a href="/wiki/Lux_Capital" title="Lux Capital">Lux Capital</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bessemer_Venture_Partners" title="Bessemer Venture Partners">Bessemer Venture Partners</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khosla_Ventures" title="Khosla Ventures">Khosla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Founders_Fund" title="Founders Fund">Founders Fund</a>, RRE Ventures and <a href="/wiki/Draper_Fisher_Jurvetson" title="Draper Fisher Jurvetson">Draper Fisher Jurvetson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ibt20151009_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-ibt20151009-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some investors see the traditional spaceflight industry as ripe for <a href="/wiki/Disruptive_innovation" title="Disruptive innovation">disruption</a>, with "a 100-fold improvement [relatively straightforward and] a thousand-fold improvement [possible]." Increasing interest by investors in economically driven spaceflight had begun to appear by 2016, and some space ventures have had to turn away investor funding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wapo20160128_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-wapo20160128-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-launched_efforts">Non-launched efforts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Non-launched efforts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Failed_spaceflight_ventures">Failed spaceflight ventures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Failed spaceflight ventures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After earlier first effort of <a href="/wiki/OTRAG" title="OTRAG">OTRAG</a>, in the 1990s the projection of a significant demand for <a href="/wiki/Communications_satellite" title="Communications satellite">communications satellite</a> launches attracted the development of a number of commercial space launch providers. The launch demand largely vanished when some of the largest satellite constellations, such as 288 satellite <a href="/wiki/Teledesic" title="Teledesic">Teledesic</a> network, were never built.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:X-33_Venture_Star_in_Orbit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1570" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/X-33_Venture_Star_in_Orbit.jpg/220px-X-33_Venture_Star_in_Orbit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/X-33_Venture_Star_in_Orbit.jpg/330px-X-33_Venture_Star_in_Orbit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/X-33_Venture_Star_in_Orbit.jpg/440px-X-33_Venture_Star_in_Orbit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:X-33_Venture_Star_in_Orbit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin" title="Lockheed Martin">Lockheed Martin</a> <a href="/wiki/VentureStar" title="VentureStar">VentureStar</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1996 <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> selected <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin" title="Lockheed Martin">Lockheed Martin</a> <a href="/wiki/Skunk_Works" title="Skunk Works">Skunk Works</a> to build the X-33 <a href="/wiki/VentureStar" title="VentureStar">VentureStar</a> prototype for a single stage to orbit (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SSTO" title="SSTO">SSTO</a>) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reusable_launch_vehicle" title="Reusable launch vehicle">reusable launch vehicle</a>. In 1999, the subscale X-33 prototype's composite liquid hydrogen fuel tank failed during testing. At project termination on 31 March 2001, NASA had funded $912 million of this wedge shaped spacecraft while Lockheed Martin financed $357 million of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/VentureStar" title="VentureStar">VentureStar</a> was to have been a full-scale commercial space transport operated by Lockheed Martin.
</p><p>In 1997 <a href="/wiki/Beal_Aerospace" title="Beal Aerospace">Beal Aerospace</a> proposed the BA-2, a low-cost heavy-lift commercial launch vehicle. On 4 March 2000, the BA-2 project tested the largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_rocket" title="Liquid rocket">liquid rocket</a> engine built since the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> In October 2000, Beal Aerospace ceased operations citing a decision by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">Department of Defense</a> to commit themselves to the development of the competing government-financed <a href="/wiki/Evolved_Expendable_Launch_Vehicle" title="Evolved Expendable Launch Vehicle">EELV</a> program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1998 <a href="/wiki/Rotary_Rocket" title="Rotary Rocket">Rotary Rocket</a> proposed the Roton, a Single Stage to Orbit (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SSTO" title="SSTO">SSTO</a>) piloted Vertical Take-off and Landing (<a href="/wiki/VTOL" title="VTOL">VTOL</a>) space transport.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> A full scale Roton Atmospheric Test Vehicle flew three times in 1999. After spending tens of millions of dollars in development the Roton failed to secure launch contracts and Rotary Rocket ceased operations in 2001.
</p><p>On 28 September 2006, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jim_Benson" title="Jim Benson">Jim Benson</a>, <a href="/wiki/SpaceDev" title="SpaceDev">SpaceDev</a> founder, announced he was founding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benson_Space_Company" title="Benson Space Company">Benson Space Company</a> with the intention of being first to market with the safest and lowest cost suborbital personal spaceflight launches, using the vertical takeoff and horizontal landing <a href="/wiki/Dream_Chaser" title="Dream Chaser">Dream Chaser</a> vehicle based on the NASA HL-20 Personnel Launch System vehicle.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The text near this tag is dated. (November 2010)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Excalibur_Almaz" title="Excalibur Almaz">Excalibur Almaz</a> had plans in 2007 to launch a modernized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TKS_Spacecraft" title="TKS Spacecraft">TKS Spacecraft</a> (for <a href="/wiki/Almaz" title="Almaz">Almaz</a> space station), for tourism and other uses. It was to feature the largest window ever on a spacecraft. Their equipment was never launched, and their hangar facility closed in 2016. It is to be converted into an educational exhibit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Escape_Dynamics" title="Escape Dynamics">Escape Dynamics</a> operated from 2010 to 2015, with the goal of making single-stage to orbit spaceplanes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20160206_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-sn20160206-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2012, the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Spike_Company" title="Golden Spike Company">Golden Spike Company</a> announced plans to privately transport space exploration participants to the surface of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> and return, beginning as early as 2020, for US$750 million per passenger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/XCOR_Aerospace" title="XCOR Aerospace">XCOR Aerospace</a> planned to initiate a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suborbital" title="Suborbital">suborbital</a> commercial spaceflight service with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lynx_rocketplane" title="Lynx rocketplane">Lynx rocketplane</a> in 2016 or 2017 at $95,000. First test flights to be conducted by 23 pilots from the Axe Apollo Space Academy, one of which is a Filipino named <a href="/wiki/Chino_Roque" title="Chino Roque">Chino Roque</a>, were planned for 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Private_space_stations">Private space stations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Private space stations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bigelow_Commercial_Space_Station" title="Bigelow Commercial Space Station">Bigelow Commercial Space Station</a></div>
<p>By 2010, <a href="/wiki/Bigelow_Aerospace" title="Bigelow Aerospace">Bigelow Aerospace</a> was developing the <i>Next-Generation Commercial Space Station</i>, a private orbital space complex.
