The <a href="/wiki/Space_station" title="Space station">space station</a> was to have been constructed of both <a href="/wiki/Sundancer" title="Sundancer">Sundancer</a> and <a href="/wiki/B330" title="B330">B330</a> expandable modules as well as a central <a href="/wiki/Docking_Compartment" title="Docking Compartment">docking node</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_propulsion" title="Spacecraft propulsion">propulsion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_panels_on_spacecraft" title="Solar panels on spacecraft">solar arrays</a>, and attached <a href="/wiki/Space_capsule" title="Space capsule">crew capsules</a>. Initial launch of space station components was planned for 2014, with portions of the station projected to be available for leased use as early as 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baocc20100715_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-baocc20100715-79">[79]</a></sup> As of  2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, no launches have taken place.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lunar_private_ventures">Lunar private ventures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Lunar private ventures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tourism_on_Moon" title="Tourism on Moon">Tourism on Moon</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Robotic_Lunar-surface_missions">Robotic Lunar-surface missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Robotic Lunar-surface missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The following companies and organizations have made initial funded launch commitments for <a href="/wiki/Google_Lunar_X_Prize" title="Google Lunar X Prize">Google Lunar X Prize</a>-related <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Lunar</a> launches in 2016:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Moon_Express" title="Moon Express">Moon Express</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lat20110408a_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-lat20110408a-80">[80]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/SpaceIL" title="SpaceIL">SpaceIL</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Synergy_Moon" title="Synergy Moon">Synergy Moon</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Team_Indus" title="Team Indus">Team Indus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hakuto" title="Hakuto">Hakuto</a> (ispace)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Private_Lunar-surface_crewed_expeditions">Private Lunar-surface crewed expeditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Private Lunar-surface crewed expeditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Shackleton_Energy_Company" title="Shackleton Energy Company">Shackleton Energy Company</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ieee200906_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-ieee200906-85">[85]</a></sup> intends to undertake human tended lunar prospecting for water ice. If significant reserves of ice are located, they plan to establish a network of "refueling service stations" in low Earth orbit and on the moon to process and provide fuel and consumables for commercial and government customers. If the prospecting is successful—ice deposits are located, the appropriate legal regime is in place to support commercial development, and the ice can be extracted — Shackleton proposes to establish a fuel-processing operation on the lunar surface and in propellant depots in Low Earth Orbit. Equipment would melt the ice and purify the water, "electrolyze the water into gaseous hydrogen and oxygen, and then condense the gases into liquid hydrogen and liquid oxygen and also process them into hydrogen peroxide, all of which could be used as rocket fuels."</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mars_ventures">Mars ventures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Private_spaceflight&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Mars ventures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In June 2012, private <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Dutch</a> non-profit <a href="/wiki/Mars_One" title="Mars One">Mars One</a> announced a private one-way (no return) human mission to <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> with the aim to establish a permanent <a href="/wiki/Colonization_of_Mars" title="Colonization of Mars">human colony on Mars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Team_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Team-86">[86]</a></sup>
The plan is to send a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communication_satellite" title="Communication satellite">communication satellite</a> and path finder lander to the planet by 2016 and, after several stages, land four humans on the Martian surface for permanent settlement in 2023.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-satellite_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-satellite-87">[87]</a></sup> A new set of four <a href="/wiki/Astronaut" title="Astronaut">astronauts</a> would then arrive every two years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reality_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reality-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Richard_Branson" title="Richard Branson">Richard Branson</a>, in his lifetime, is "determined to be a part of starting a population on Mars. I think its absolutely realistic. It will happen...I think over the next 20 years, we will take literally hundreds of thousands of people to space and that will give us the financial resources to do even bigger things"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2013, the US nonprofit <a href="/wiki/Inspiration_Mars_Foundation" title="Inspiration Mars Foundation">Inspiration Mars Foundation</a> announced a plan to send a married couple on a 2018 mission to travel to Mars and back to Earth on a 501-day round trip, with no landing planned on Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pm20130227_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-pm20130227-90">[90]</a></sup>
The mission will take advantage of an infrequently occurring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_return_trajectory" title="Free return trajectory">free return trajectory</a>—a unique orbit opportunity which occurs only once every fifteen years—and will allow the <a href="/wiki/Space_capsule" title="Space capsule">space capsule</a> to use the smallest possible amount of <a href="/wiki/Rocket_propellant" title="Rocket propellant">fuel</a> to get it to Mars and back to Earth.
The two-person American crew – a man and a woman – will orbit around Mars at a distance of 100 miles (160 km) of the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-awst20130304_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-awst20130304-91">[91]</a></sup>
"If anything goes wrong, the spacecraft should make its own way back to Earth — but with no possibility of any short-cuts home."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-independent20130228_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-independent20130228-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-bromberg1999-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-bromberg1999_18-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-bromberg1999_18-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Bromberg, Joan Lisa (1999). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=-UebVg1YqsoC&amp;pg=PA186" rel="nofollow"><i>NASA and the Space Industry</i></a>. Johns Hopkins University Press. p. 186. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8018-6532-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8018-6532-9">978-0-8018-6532-9</a>. <q><i>On the other hand, NASA resisted the buildup of a commercial launch industry. Launching was for many years an enterprise that was run by a de facto partnership of NASA and the companies from which NASA bought launchers and launch services. NASA proposed to put an end to that enterprise in the 1980s; it sought to enthrone the shuttle as the nation's commercial, as well as government, launcher. The prospect of erecting a private sector launch industry alongside the NASA shuttle was discussed, but it did not become a reality because the shuttle was too tough a competitor for private vehicles. Only the grounding of the shuttle after the Challenger accident allowed the commercial launch industry to get started.</i></q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=NASA+and+the+Space+Industry&amp;rft.pages=186&amp;rft.pub=Johns+Hopkins+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1999&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-8018-6532-9&amp;rft.aulast=Bromberg&amp;rft.aufirst=Joan+Lisa&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D-UebVg1YqsoC%26pg%3DPA186&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APrivate+spaceflight"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-usg19981028-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-usg19981028_22-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-usg19981028_22-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news"><a class="external text" href="http://www.nasa.gov/offices/ogc/commercial/CommercialSpaceActof1998.html" rel="nofollow">"Public Law 105-303: Commercial Space Act of 1998"</a>. <i>NASA Office of the General Counsel - Reference</i>. NASA. 28 October 1998. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20150202023518/http://www.nasa.gov/offices/ogc/commercial/CommercialSpaceActof1998.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 2 February 2015<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">25 December</span> 2014</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=NASA+Office+of+the+General+Counsel+-+Reference&amp;rft.atitle=Public+Law+105-303%3A+Commercial+Space+Act+of+1998&amp;rft.date=1998-10-28&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.nasa.gov%2Foffices%2Fogc%2Fcommercial%2FCommercialSpaceActof1998.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APrivate+spaceflight"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.asi.org/adb/j/02/legal-roadblocks.html" rel="nofollow">It's illegal for private enterprise to go into space.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/19990218062255/http://www.asi.org/adb/j/02/legal-roadblocks.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 1999-02-18 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a><br/> U.S. law regulates private space launches, and the current law is quite unreasonable. A private company wanting to build and launch a rocket faces a mountain of red tape and very long lead times for getting approval from the government. However, although these problems add a tremendous amount of cost to private space launches, there are no laws that prohibit private space launches.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-nbc2004-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-nbc2004_24-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-nbc2004_24-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.nbcnews.com/id/6682611/ns/technology_and_science-space/t/private-spaceflight-bill-signed-law/" rel="nofollow">Private Spaceflight Bill Signed into Law</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20141231001919/http://www.nbcnews.com/id/6682611/ns/technology_and_science-space/t/private-spaceflight-bill-signed-law/" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 31 December 2014 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a><br/>The Commercial Space Launch Amendments Act, or H.R. 5382, placed a clear legislative stamp on regulations that were being formulated by the Federal Aviation Administration. Among other provisions, the law was intended to let paying passengers fly on suborbital launch vehicles at their own risk.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-tsr20150526-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-tsr20150526_25-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-tsr20150526_25-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-tsr20150526_25-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news">Foust, Jeff (26 May 2015). <a class="external text" href="http://www.thespacereview.com/article/2759/1" rel="nofollow">"Congress launches commercial space legislation"</a>. <i>The Space Review</i>. Space News. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20150527063504/http://www.thespacereview.com/article/2759/1" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 27 May 2015<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2 June</span> 2015</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Space+Review&amp;rft.atitle=Congress+launches+commercial+space+legislation&amp;rft.date=2015-05-26&amp;rft.aulast=Foust&amp;rft.aufirst=Jeff&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.thespacereview.com%2Farticle%2F2759%2F1&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APrivate+spaceflight"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.jaunted.com/story/2014/1/29/17284/7200/travel/Wow.+FAA+Paperwork+Delays+May+Block+Virgin+Galactic+Debut." rel="nofollow">Wow. FAA Paperwork Delays May Block Virgin Galactic Debut.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20141231035525/http://www.jaunted.com/story/2014/1/29/17284/7200/travel/Wow.+FAA+Paperwork+Delays+May+Block+Virgin+Galactic+Debut." rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 31 December 2014 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a><br/>Apparently the problem is that the FAA hasn't given Virgin Atlantic a commercial operator's license (at least in part because no one has set the rules for what counts as safe spaceflight). Without a commercial operator's license, it's illegal for anyone - including Virgin's billionaire owner Richard Branson - to be a passenger on space planes. Think about this for a second: the private sector will have created a plane that glides through space, just over a century after heavier than air flight was even invented, but the scolds at the FAA need extra time to fill out their paperwork. Until then, apparently, the future can't happen.</span>
</li>
